Kitabah of Mudabbarah
Ruling on kitabah of mudabbarah and her options upon master's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on kitabah of a mudabbarah and explain her options when the master dies insolvent, according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Kitabah of a mudabbarah is valid; if the master dies with no wealth, she works for two-thirds of her value.

This passage from Al-Quduri addresses a specific scenario where a master grants both tadbir (promise of freedom upon death) and kitabah (contract for self-purchase) to the same slave woman. The Hanafi school validates this combination but introduces a crucial option for the slave when the master's estate cannot fulfill the tadbir commitment. Understanding this ruling requires knowing the interplay between tadbir's one-third limitation and the kitabah contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating that if the master makes kitabah with his mudabbarah, it is valid (ja'iz). Note that this is a ruling of permissibility and validity.
2
Identify the condition triggering the option
The trigger is the master's death with no wealth (i.e., the estate is insufficient to cover the one-third of the tadbir).
3
State the options given to the mudabbarah
The mudabbarah has a choice: either to work for two-thirds of her market value or to work for the full amount of the kitabah contract.
The two-thirds corresponds to the portion that would have been freed from tadbir if the estate had one-third.
4
Identify the preferred ruling according to Abu Hanifa
The text concludes that according to Abu Hanifa, she works for two-thirds of her value. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
Other opinions may exist within the school, but the text only cites Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Note the practical consequence
If the mudabbarah pays two-thirds of her value, she becomes free. If she chooses to pay the full kitabah amount, she also becomes free but pays more.
The option protects her from being forced to pay the full kitabah when the tadbir portion is unfulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَاتَبَ مُدَبَّرَتَهُ جَازَ فَإِنْ مَاتَ الْمَوْلَى وَلَا مَالَ لَهُ كَانَتْ بِالْخِيَارِ وَبَيْنَ أَنْ تَسْعَى فِي ثُلُثَيْ قِيمَتِهَا أَوْ فِي جَمِيعِ مَالِ الْكِتَابَةِ تَسْعَى فِي ثُلُثَيْ قِيمَتِهَا
And if he makes kitabah with his mudabbarah, it is valid. If the master dies and has no wealth, she has the choice between working for two-thirds of her value or for all of the kitabah money; she works for two-thirds of her value.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This ruling from Al-Quduri deals with a mudabbarah (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death). The master may validly contract kitabah with her. If the master dies insolvent (with no other wealth to cover the tadbir), the mudabbarah has an option: she may either work to pay two-thirds of her market value (which is the portion of her freedom that was promised, since one-third of the estate is free for tadbir but the master's death left no wealth to enforce it), or she may work to pay the full kitabah amount. According to Abu Hanifa, she works for two-thirds of her value. The text mentions this is the position of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
مدبرة (mudabbarah): A female slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a master dies with no wealth after making kitabah with his mudabbarah, what does Abu Hanifa rule she must do?
A) She must pay the full kitabah amount
B) She must pay two-thirds of her value
C) She is freed without payment
D) She becomes umm walad

Key Takeaway
Kitabah of a mudabbarah is valid; upon the master's death with no wealth, she has the option to work for two-thirds of her value according to Abu Hanifa.

Mukatab Cannot Free His Slave or Give Hibah with Compensation
Ruling from Al-Quduri with hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a mukatab's act of freeing his slave for money or giving a gift with compensation is invalid, and cite the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
A mukatab's incomplete ownership restricts him from transactions that could jeopardize his kitabah.

The mukatab (a slave under kitabah contract) has a unique legal status: he owns property but his capacity is limited to matters directly related to earning his freedom. This lesson examines why he cannot free his own slave for a payment or give a gift in exchange for something (hibah bi 'iwad). The ruling is grounded in a hadith that explicitly restricts such actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the person and the act
Read the Arabic text carefully. Who is the subject? (al-mukatab). What two acts are mentioned? (freeing a slave for money, and giving a gift with compensation).
2
State the ruling for each act
For the first act: 'lam yajuz' (not permissible). For the second: 'lam yasihh' (not valid). Both are invalid.
In Hanafi terms, these actions are batil (void).
3
Identify the evidence (dalil)
The text's commentary cites a hadith: 'The mukatab cannot free nor give except with the permission of his master.' This is the primary proof.
The hadith is from Bayhaqi, classed as zanni al-thubut.
4
Understand the underlying legal reason
The mukatab's ownership is incomplete; he is still a slave in some respects. Acts that could diminish his assets or complicate his kitabah are restricted.
The usul note: he cannot perform actions unrelated to trade or its accessories.
5
Apply to similar transactions
If a mukatab attempts other wealth-depleting transactions (e.g., selling a slave at a loss), similar restrictions apply unless linked to his trade.
Always check if the act is necessary for earning his freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا أَعْتَقَ الْمُكَاتَبُ عَبْدَهُ عَلَى مَالٍ لَمْ يَجُزْ وَإِنْ وَهَبَ عَلَى عِوَضٍ لَمْ يَصِحَّ
And when the mukatab frees his slave in exchange for money, it is not permissible. And if he gives a gift with compensation, it is not valid.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This is a ruling from Al-Quduri stating that a mukatab (a slave under kitabah contract) cannot validly free his own slave (if he owns one) for a payment, nor can he give a gift with compensation (hibah bi 'iwad). The reason is that the mukatab's ownership is incomplete; his actions that could affect his own freedom or property rights are restricted. The text includes a hadith evidence from Hasan al-Basri that a mukatab cannot free or give except with the master's permission. The usul note at the bottom states: 'A mukatab cannot perform actions that are not related to trade or its accessories, or hire or its accessories.' This underscores that the mukatab's capacity is limited to what directly pertains to his kitabah.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitabah contract to earn his freedom by paying installments.
هبة على عوض (hibah 'ala 'iwad): A gift given in exchange for something, essentially a sale-like transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a mukatab tries to free his own slave in exchange for money?
A) It is valid and the slave becomes free
B) It is not permissible (invalid)
C) It is valid with the master's permission only
D) The slave becomes free but the mukatab is punished

Key Takeaway
The mukatab's limited capacity prohibits wealth-depleting transactions like freeing slaves for money or giving gifts with compensation, as they are not related to his kitabah.

Mukatab Making Kitabah with His Own Slave: Validity and Wala'
Ruling on a mukatab making kitabah with his own slave and effect on wala'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a mukatab making kitabah with his own slave and determine the effect on wala' based on the timing of payment.

Core Idea
A mukatab's kitabah with his own slave is valid; wala' flows to the free emancipator at the moment of payment.

This lesson covers a layered case: a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to buy his freedom) who owns a slave and enters a kitabah contract with that slave. The validity of the contract is established, but the wala' (clientship) of the second slave depends on the timing of emancipation. The Hanafi school bases this ruling on the principle that wala' can only be held by a free person, leading to conditional outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is the act? A mukatab making kitabah with his own slave. The ruling: it is valid (jāz).
The act is a contract of kitabah between a mukatab and his slave.
2
Understand the Conditional Scenarios
The ruling presents two scenarios based on who pays off their kitabah first: the second slave (B) pays before the first (A) becomes free, or B pays after A becomes free.
This timing determines the outcome.
3
Trace the Legal Effect on Wala'
In the first scenario, wala' of B goes to the original master. In the second, B becomes free and his wala' belongs to A.
The key principle: wala' can only be held by a free person.
4
Connect to the Evidence
The author cites the practice of the predecessors (Ata') as a Qawl Sahabi: the slave's estate goes to the one who made kitabah with him.
This supports the validity and the conditional wala' assignment.
5
Apply the Principle
Wala' is only for free persons. At the time of B's payment, if A is not yet free, the original master is the only free person and thus gains wala'. If A is free, he is the direct emancipator and gains wala'.
This explains the conditional outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَاتَبَ عَبْدَهُ جَازَ فَإِنْ أَدَّى الثَّانِي قَبْلَ أَنْ يَعْتِقَ الْأَوَّلُ فَوَلاؤُهُ لِلْمَوْلَى وَإِنْ أَدَّى الثَّانِي بَعْدَ عِتْقِ الْأَوَّلِ عَتَقَ وَوَلَاؤُهُ لَهُ
And if he (the mukatab) makes kitabah with his slave, it is valid. If the second (slave) pays before the first (mukatab) becomes free, then his wala' belongs to the (original) master. And if the second pays after the first becomes free, he becomes free and his wala' belongs to him (the first, now free).
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This ruling addresses a mukatab who owns a slave and enters a kitabah contract with that slave. The contract is valid. The wala' (right of inheritance and loyalty) depends on the timing of payment: if the second slave pays off his kitabah before the first (the original mukatab) becomes free, then the wala' of the second goes to the original master (the one who wrote the first kitabah). This is because wala' can only be established for a free person, and the first is still a slave. If the second pays after the first has become free (by completing his own kitabah), then the second becomes free and his wala' belongs to the first (now free), as he is the emancipator. The text includes a hadith from Ata' about this scenario. The usul notes state: 'A mukatab can make kitabah with his slave' and 'Wala' is for free persons, not for slaves.'

Key Terms
الولاء (al-wala'): Clientship, a bond of loyalty and inheritance between an emancipator and the freed slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mukatab (A) makes kitabah with his slave (B), and B pays off his kitabah before A becomes free, to whom does B's wala' belong?
A) To A
B) To the original master (the one who made kitabah with A)
C) To no one
D) To B himself

Key Takeaway
The wala' of a slave freed via kitabah belongs to the actual free emancipator at the time of freedom; timing is decisive.

Wala' Belongs to the Emancipator and the Condition of Sa'ibah is Void
Opening of Kitab al-Wala' stating wala' belongs to the emancipator and condition of sa'ibah is void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that wala' belongs to the emancipator and explain why any condition of sa'ibah is void, citing the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Wala' is an inseparable right of the emancipator — no condition can nullify it.

The Book of Wala' (Clientship) establishes a fundamental ruling of Islamic law regarding the bond between an emancipator and a freed slave. This lesson examines the opening passage from al-Quduri, which states the core rule: wala' belongs to the one who frees, and any stipulation to make the freed slave a 'sa'ibah' (without patronage) is void. We will also explore the hadith evidence that supports this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is emancipation ('itq). The ruling is that wala' (patronage) belongs to the emancipator. This applies equally to men and women.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites hadiths: the Prophet forbade selling or giving wala' (Bukhari 2535), and 'the patron of a people is from themselves' (Bukhari 6761). These prove wala' is a fixed right.
The hadith are ahad but sufficient for this ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition of transferring wala' indicates it is firmly attached to the emancipator. The condition of sa'ibah would effectively nullify or transfer the wala', so it is void.
The condition contradicts the shar'i purpose of emancipation.
4
Note the consensus
This ruling is agreed upon across all Sunni schools; there is no legitimate ikhtilaf on the principle that wala' follows the emancipator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الْوَلَاءِ قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ - ل إذَا أَعْتَقَ الرَّجُلُ مَمْلُوكَهُ فَوَلَاؤُهُ لَهُ وَكَذَلِكَ الْمَرْأَةُ تَعْتِقُ فَإِنْ شَرَطَ أَنَّهُ سَائِبَةٌ فَالشَّرْطُ بَاطِلٌ ، وَالْوَلَاءُ لِمَنْ أَعْتَقَ
The Book of Wala'. He (the author) said - may Allah have mercy on him -: When a man frees his slave, his wala' belongs to him. Similarly, a woman frees. If he stipulates that he is a sa'ibah, the condition is void, and the wala' belongs to the one who freed.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This opens the Book of Wala' (Clientship). The ruling is that when a person (man or woman) frees a slave, the wala' (right of inheritance and allegiance) belongs to that emancipator. If the emancipator stipulates that the freed slave is a 'sa'ibah' (meaning he is released without any obligations or connection, as was done in pre-Islamic times), that condition is null and void, and the wala' remains with the emancipator. The text provides several proofs: a hadith from Ibn Umar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited selling or giving wala' (Bukhari 2535); a hadith from Anas that 'the patron of a people is from themselves' (Bukhari 6761); and a saying of Tamim al-Dari (raised as marfu'). These establish that wala' is a right that cannot be alienated.

Key Terms
السائبة (al-sa'ibah): A freed slave with no ties of patronage, as in pre-Islamic custom; condition void in Islam.
الولاء (al-wala'): Patronage right; the bond of loyalty and inheritance between the emancipator and freed slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man frees his slave and stipulates that the slave is sa'ibah (with no patronage), what is the ruling?
A) The condition is valid and the slave has no patron
B) The condition is void and wala' belongs to the emancipator
C) The slave becomes free and wala' goes to the Muslim community
D) The emancipation is invalid

Key Takeaway
The bond of wala' is an inviolable right that follows emancipation; any condition to nullify or transfer it is void.

Wala' of a Mukatab: Payment and Posthumous Freedom
Ruling on wala' of a mukatab after payment or posthumous freedom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on wala' of a mukatab after payment or posthumous freedom and identify the hadith proof that wala' belongs to the one who frees.

Core Idea
Wala' always follows the one who frees, even after the master's death.

This lesson addresses the wala' (clientship) of a mukatab (slave under kitabah contract) when he completes his payments or is freed after his master's death. The ruling is straightforward but has a significant implication: the master's death does not nullify the wala'; it transfers to his heirs. The proof comes from the well-known hadith that wala' belongs to the one who frees.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling for payment
Identify that when the mukatab pays his kitabah, he becomes free and his wala' belongs to his master.
2
Extend the ruling for posthumous freedom
Note that if the mukatab is freed after the master's death (e.g., by the master's will or heirs), his wala' goes to the master's heirs.
3
Identify the proof
The hadith 'إنما الولاء لمن أعتق' (wala' belongs to the one who frees) proves that the emancipator (or his estate) retains wala'.
This is a sahih hadith from Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Apply the proof
Since the master (or his estate) is the cause of freedom through kitabah, the wala' is tied to him and passes to his heirs upon his death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذْ أَدَّى الْمُكَاتَبُ عَتَقَ وَوَلَاؤُهُ لِلْمَوْلَى. وَكَذَا إِنْ أُعْتِقَ بَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْمَوْلَى فَوَلَاؤُهُ لِوَرَثَةِ الْمَوْلَى
And when the mukatab pays (his kitabah), he becomes free and his wala' belongs to the master. Similarly, if he is freed after the master's death, his wala' belongs to the heirs of the master.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This continues the Book of Wala'. When a mukatab completes his payments, he becomes free and his wala' (clientship) belongs to his former master (the one who wrote the kitabah). If the mukatab is freed (by other means, e.g., the master's heirs freeing him) after the master's death, his wala' goes to the master's heirs. The text provides proofs: a hadith from Aisha (ra) that the Prophet said 'wala' belongs to the one who frees' (Bukhari 2560/Muslim 1504), and sayings of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and 'Amir (al-Sha'bi) that even if the emancipation is sa'ibah, the wala' is for the one who freed. The usul note explains: 'Wala' situations: if a slave is freed and then dies, his wealth goes to the one who freed; if brotherhood is established, that person inherits.'

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave under kitabah contract; upon payment becomes free with wala' to master.
الولاء (al-wala'): Clientship/patronage relationship established by emancipation; includes inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mukatab completes his payments after the master has died, to whom does his wala' belong?
A) To the master even though he is dead
B) To the master's heirs
C) To the Muslim ruler
D) To no one; the mukatab is free with no wala'

Key Takeaway
Wala' follows the one who frees; upon the master's death, the right passes to his heirs.

Types of Erroneous Killing (Khat') and Their Rulings
Defines two types of erroneous killing and their consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of erroneous killing (khat') according to the Hanafi school and state the rulings (kaffarah, diyah, no qisas) with evidence from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
In erroneous killing, there is no qisas but wajib kaffarah and heavy diyah on the 'aqilah.

This passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar defines the categories of unintentional homicide and their legal consequences. Understanding the distinction between error in intent (khat' fi al-qasd) and error in action (khat' fi al-fi'l) is crucial for applying the correct rulings of expiation and compensation. We will examine the definitions, the obligations, and the proofs from Quran and hadith that established these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State that the passage deals with erroneous killing (khat'). The ruling is that there is no qisas (retaliation) but rather wajib kaffarah (expiation) and heavy diyah (blood money) on the 'aqilah (male agnates of the killer).
The heavy diyah is specified as 'al-mughallazah'.
2
Distinguish the two types of khat'
Read the definitions: (1) Error in intent: one intends to shoot a permissible target (like game) but mistakenly hits a human. (2) Error in action: one intends to shoot a target (like a bird) but the projectile accidentally hits a human.
In both cases, there is no sinful intent.
3
List the obligations
The obligations are: kaffarah (freeing a believing slave or fasting two consecutive months) and diyah upon the 'aqilah. There is no sin (ma'tham) because the act was unintentional.
Kaffarah is a personal obligation; diyah is paid by the 'aqilah.
4
Examine the evidence
The Quranic verse (4:92) is cited: 'Whoever kills a believer by mistake, then (the atonement is) freeing a believing slave and diyah delivered to his family.' Also hadith from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and Ali specify the division of diyah into categories of camels.
The proofs establish the obligation of kaffarah and diyah.
5
Note the role of 'aqilah
The diyah is not paid by the killer personally but by his 'aqilah (male agnates). This is a Hanafi rule based on solidarity and the absence of criminal intent.
The 'aqilah are liable for diyah in cases of khat' only, not in intentional killing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الْحِنَايَاتِ وَالْكَفَّارَةُ وَلا قَوَدَ فِيهِ وَفِيهِ الدِّيَةُ الْمُغَلَّظَةُ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ الْقَتْلُ الْخَطَأُ وَالْخَطَأُ عَلَى وَجْهَيْنِ خَطَاً فِي الْقَصْدِ وَهُوَ أَنْ يَرْمِيَ شَخْصًا يَظُنُّهُ صَيْدًا فَإِذَا هُوَ آدَمِيٌّ وَخَطَةٌ فِي الْفِعْلِ وَهُوَ أَنْ يَرْمِيَ غَرَضًا فَيُصِيبُ آدَمِيًّا وَمُوجَبُ ذَلِكَ الْكَفَّارَةُ، وَالدِّيَةُ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ وَلَا مَأْثَمَ فِيهِ شريف، بَابُ جَنِينِ الْمَرْأَةِ وَأَنَّ الْعَقْلَ عَلَى الْوَالِدِ وَعَصَبَةِ الْوَالِدِ لَا عَلَى الْوَلَدِ، نمبر 6910 مسلم شريف بَاب ثُبُوتِ الْقِصَاصِ فِي الْقَتْلِ بِالحَجَرِ وَغَيْرِهِ مِنَ الْمُحَدَّدَاتِ وَالْمُثَقَلَاتِ، وَقَتْلِ الرَّجُلِ بِالْمَرْأَةِ، غير 1682) د وجه (۱) قول الصحابي لثبوت الْقَتْلُ الْخَطَأَ وَاخْطَأُ عَلَى وَجْهَيْنِ \ قَالَ عَبْدُ اللَّهِ: «فِي شِبْهِ الْعَمْدِ خَمْسٌ وَعِشْرُونَ حِقَّةً، وَخَمْسٌ وَعِشْرُونَ جَدَعَةً، وَخَمْسٌ وَعِشْرُونَ بَنَاتِ لَبُونِ، وَخَمْسٌ وَعِشْرُونَ بَنَاتِ فَخَاضِ»، سنن ابوداود، بَابٌ فِي دِيَةِ الْخَطَا شِبْهِ الْعَمْدِ، نمبر 4552/سنن ترمذی، بَابُ مَا جَاءَ فِي الدِّيَةِ كَمْ هِيَ مِنَ الإِبل، نمبر 1386) وجه : (۲) قول الصحابي لثبوت الْقَتْلُ الْخَطَأَ وَالْخَطَأُ عَلَى وَجْهَيْنِ عَنْ عَاصِمِ بْنِ ضَمْرَةَ، قَالَ: قَالَ عَلِيٌّ : فِي الْخَطَا أَرْبَاعًا خَمْسٌ وَعِشْرُونَ حِقَّةً، وَخَمْسٌ وَعِشْرُونَ جَذَعَةً، وَخَمْسٌ وَعِشْرُونَ بَنَاتِ لَبُونِ، وَخَمْسٌ وَعِشْرُونَ بَنَاتِ فَخَاضِ (سن ،ابوداود، بَابٌ فِي دِيَةِ الْخَطَا شِبْهِ الْعَمْدِ، نمبر 4553) كوجه (۱) قول التابعي لثبوت وَخَطَةٌ فِي الْفِعْلِ وَهُوَ أَنْ يَرْمِيَ غَرَضًا \ عَنْ مَعْمَرٍ قَالَ: «كَتَبَ عُمَرُ بْنُ عَبْدِ الْعَزِيزِ فِي الْخَطَا أَنْ يُرِيدَ امْرَأَ فَيُصِيبَ غَيْرَهُ»، (مصنف عبدالرزاق، بَابُ الخطا، غمبر 17209) دوجه: (ا) الآية لثبوت وَمُوجَبُ ذَلِكَ الْكَفَّارَةُ، ال﴿وَمَن قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنًا خَطَئًا فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ إِلَّا أَن يَصَّدَّقُوا) (سورة النساء ، 4 أيت، نمبر 92) وجه (۲) الحديث لثبوت وَمُوجَبُ ذَلِكَ الْكَفَّارَةُ، وَالدِّيَةُ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ أَعَنْ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ بْن مَسْعُودٍ، قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم : « فِي دِيَةِ الْطَ عِشْرُونَ حِقَّةَ، وَعِشْرُونَ جَذَعَةً، وَعِشْرُونَ بِنْتَ فَخَاضِ، اصول: قتل کی تیسری قسم قتل خطا میں قصاص لازم نہیں ہوتا ہے ، بلکہ صرف دیت اور کفارہ ہے۔ اصول : قتل خطایہ ہے کہ کسی اور کو مارنا چاہتا تھا لیکن غلطی سے کسی اور کو لگ گیا ہو۔
The Book of Injuries: And the expiation, and there is no qisas in it, and in it is the heavy diyah upon the 'aqilah. Erroneous killing, and error is of two types: error in intent, which is that he shoots a person thinking it is game, then it is a human; and error in action, which is that he shoots at a target and hits a human. The obligation of that is expiation, and diyah upon the 'aqilah, and there is no sin in it. ... (then proofs from hadith and Quran)
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Jinayat. It first states the ruling that in erroneous killing (khat') there is no qisas but rather an increased diyah (heavy diyah) on the 'aqilah (the male agnates of the killer). It then defines two types of khat': (1) error in intent (khat' fi al-qasd): one intends to shoot a permissible target (like game) but mistakenly hits a human; (2) error in action (khat' fi al-fi'l): one intends to shoot a target (like a bird) but the projectile hits a human. In both cases, the consequences are: kaffarah (expiation: freeing a slave or fasting) and diyah on the 'aqilah, and no sin is incurred because there was no intent. The passage then provides evidence: a narration from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (a saying of the Prophet on diyah amounts), from Ali ibn Abi Talib, from caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, and the Quranic verse (4:92) that prescribes freeing a believing slave and diyah for accidental killing.

Key Terms
خَطَأ (khat'): Unintentional killing: either error in intent or error in action.
عَاقِلَة ('āqilah): The male agnates (paternal relatives) of the killer who are liable for paying diyah.
دِيَة (diyah): Monetary compensation paid to the heirs of the victim for homicide or bodily injury.
كَفَّارَة (kaffārah): Expiation for accidental killing: freeing a believing slave, or fasting two consecutive months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what are the two types of erroneous killing (khat')?
A) Intentional and mistaken
B) Error in intent and error in action
C) Direct and indirect
D) With weapon and without weapon

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school categorizes erroneous killing into two types, both incurring wajib kaffarah and heavy diyah on the 'aqilah, with no qisas and no sin.

Killing by Cause and Analogous Cases
Ruling on killing by cause (digging well, placing stone) and similar cases like sleeper rolling over

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing by cause (qatl bi sabab) and distinguish it from direct erroneous killing in terms of diyah, kaffarah, and the role of the 'aqilah.

Core Idea
Killing by cause requires diyah on the 'aqilah but no kaffarah — absence of direct action changes the obligation.

This passage from al-Quduri addresses cases where death results indirectly from an action, such as digging a well on another's property or a sleeping person rolling over. The Hanafi school classifies these as killing by cause (qatl bi sabab) with a distinct ruling from direct erroneous killing (qatl khat'). Understanding this distinction is essential for proper application of diyah and kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Determine whether the killing is direct (mubashir) or by cause (sabab). In the passage, the sleeper rolling over and the digger of a well are examples of cause.
Direct = immediate act; cause = indirect, e.g., setting a trap.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
For killing by cause, the ruling is diyah on the 'aqilah (not on the perpetrator) and no kaffarah.
Diyah is wajib; kaffarah is not required.
3
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Ibn Abbas equating such killings to erroneous killing (khat') and the hadith of jubar (well, mine) from Abu Hurayrah.
The proofs are ahad and zanni in transmission.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith shows that the diyah is like that of error, but the absence of direct intent excludes kaffarah.
The 'aql (diyah) is likened to khat', but kaffarah is not mentioned.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Some schools may require kaffarah; the Hanafi distinguishes because the act is indirect.
Practical consequence: in a Hanafi court, only diyah is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا قَتَلَ النَّائِمُ رَجُلًا بِانْقِلَابِهِ فَحُكْمُهُ حُكْمُ الْخَطَإِ وَمَا أُجْرِيَ فَجَرَى الْخَطَأَ مِثْلُ النَّائِمِ يَنْقَلِبُ عَلَى رَجُلٍ فَيَقْتُلُهُ فَحُكْمُهُ حُكْمُ الْخَطَإِ وَأَمَّا الْقَتْلُ بِسَبَبٍ كَحَافِرِ الْبِئْرِ وَوَاضِعِ الْحَجَرِ فِي غَيْرِ مِلْكِهِ الْحَدِيثُ مُوجِبُ ذَلِكَ إِذَا تَلِفَ فِيهِ آدَمِيٌّ الدِّيَةُ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ وَلَا كَفَّارَةَ فِيهِ وَوَاضِعُ الْحَجَرِ وَالْقِصَاصُ وَاجِبٌ بِقَتْلِ كُلِّ تَحْقُونِ الدَّمِ عَلَى التَّأْبِيدِ وَعِشْرُونَ بِنْتَ لَبُونِ، وَعِشْرُونَ بَنِي فَخَاضٍ ذُكٌرٍ»»، (سنن ابوداود، بَابُ الدِّيَةِ كَمْ هي، نمبر 4545/ سنن ترمذى، بَابُ مَا جَاءَ فِي الدِّيَةِ كَمْ هِيَ مِنَ الإِبِلِ، نمبر 1386) ووجه (۱) الحديث لثبوت وَمَا أُجْرِيَ فَجَرَى الْخَطَأَ مِثْلُ النَّائِمِ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسِ قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مَنْ قُتِلَ فِي عِمِّيَّا أَوْ رِمِيًّا بِحَجَرٍ أَوْ عَصًا أَوْ بِسَوْطٍ فَعَقْلُهُ عَقْلُ خَطَإٍ»، (سنن دارقطني، كِتَابُ الْحُدُودِ وَالدِّيَاتِ وَغَيْرُهُ، نمبر 3137/سنن ابي داود بَابُ مَنْ قُتِلَ فِي عِمِّيَّا بَيْنَ قَوْمٍ، نمبر 4539) وجه: (1) الحديث لثبوت وَأَمَّا الْقَتْلُ بِسَبَبٍ كَحَافِرِ الْبِئْرِ عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ : أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «الْعَجْمَاءُ جَرْحُهَا جُبَارٌ، وَالْبِئْرُ جُبَارٌ، وَالْمَعْدِنُ جُبَارٌ، وَفِي الرِّكَازِ الْخُمُس»، (بخاري شريف، بَابُ : الْمَعْدِنُ جُبَارٌ وَالْبِئْرُ جُبَارٌ، نمبر 6912) وجه : (۲) قول التابعى لثبوت وَأَمَّا الْقَتْلُ بِسَبَبٍ كَحَافِرِ الْبِئْرِ عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ، قَالَ: «مَنْ حَفَرَ فِي غَيْرِ بِنَائِهِ، أَوْ بَنَى فِي غَيْرِ سَمَائِهِ، فَقَدْ ضَمِنَ»، (مصنف عبدالرزاق، بَابُ الْجُدُرِ الْمَائِلِ وَالطَّرِيقِ، نمبر 18409/مصنف ابن ابي شيبه، الرَّجُلُ يُخْرِجُ مِنْ حَدِهِ شَيْئًا فَيُصِيبُ إِنْسَانًا، نمبر 27354) الوجه: (ا) قول التابعى لثبوت وَمُوجَبُ ذَلِكَ إِذَا تَلِفَ فِيهِ آدَمِيٌّ الدِّيَةُ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ، قَالَ: «مَنْ حَفَرَ فِي غَيْرِ بِنَائِهِ، أَوْ بَنَى فِي غَيْرِ سَمَائِهِ، فَقَدْ ضَمِنَ»،(مصنف عبدالرزاق، بَابُ الْجُدُرِ الْمَائِلِ وَالطَّرِيقِ، نمبر 18409/مصنف ابن ابي شيبه، الرَّجُلُ يُخْرِجُ مِنْ حَدِهِ شَيْئًا فَيُصِيبُ إِنْسَانًا، نمبر 27354) اصول: سبب کے طور پر جرم کیا تو دیت دینی ہو گی، کفارہ نہیں لازم ہو گا۔ اصول: جان میں قتل عمد اور شبہ عمد ہے، عضو میں شبہ عمد نہیں ہے، وہاں لکڑی سے بھی عضو نقصان کرے گا تو وہ عمدہ ہی ہو گا اور اس پر قصاص لازم ہو گا۔
And if a sleeping person kills a man by his turning over, his ruling is the ruling of error. And what is run so it runs as error, like a sleeping person who turns over upon a man and kills him, his ruling is the ruling of error. As for killing by cause, such as the digger of a well and the placer of a stone in someone else's property... the obligation of that—if a human is destroyed in it—is diyah upon the 'aqilah, and there is no expiation in it. And the placer of a stone, and qisas is obligatory for the killing of every bloodshed perpetually...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on killing by cause and analogous cases. It first states that if a sleeping person rolls over and kills someone, it is treated as erroneous killing (khat') with the same ruling: diyah on the 'aqilah and kaffarah. Then it discusses killing by cause (qatl bi sabab), such as digging a well on someone else's property or placing a stone there that causes death. In such cases, the ruling is that diyah is due on the 'aqilah, but there is no kaffarah because the act was not directly killing. The text then provides evidence: a hadith from Ibn Abbas that killing in confusion with a stone or stick is treated as error, and a hadith from Abu Hurayrah that wounds from an animal, a well, and a mine are 'jubar' (free of liability), meaning diyah is not due on the owner in some cases? Actually, the hadith is used to show that the digger is liable. Also, a saying of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that one who digs on another's property is liable. The Urdu commentary adds that in killing by cause, diyah is due but no kaffarah, and that in injuries to limbs, there is no shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional); any injury with a stick is considered intentional and subject to qisas.

Key Terms
قَتْلٌ بِسَبَبٍ (qatl bi sabab): Killing that results indirectly from an action like digging a well or placing an obstruction, where the death is caused by the thing, not directly by the actor.
جُبَارٌ (jubār): Free of liability; no compensation is owed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for killing by cause (e.g., digging a well in someone else's property that causes death)?
A) Both diyah and kaffarah are required
B) Only diyah is required on the 'aqilah, no kaffarah
C) Only kaffarah is required
D) No liability because it is unintentional

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, killing by cause (e.g., digging a well) obligates diyah on the 'aqilah but no kaffarah, differing from direct erroneous killing which requires both.

When One Cuts the Right Hands of Two Men
Two rulings with proofs on qisas and diya for cutting hands of multiple victims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a single perpetrator cuts the hand of two victims, distinguishing between when both victims are present versus when only one is present, and explain the proofs.

Core Idea
When qisas for a single limb cannot be divided among multiple victims, combine qisas with proportional diya.

This passage from Quduri presents a nuanced scenario in jinayat: a single perpetrator cuts the right hand of two men. Since the hand is one limb and cannot be split in qisas, the law provides a combined remedy. Understanding this ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle of combining qisas and diya when necessary, and the proofs from sunnah and the practice of the salaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct cases: one where both victims are present, and one where only one is present. Identify the remedy in each.
The ruling changes depending on presence of victims.
2
Dissect the remedies
For each scenario, list exactly what the victims receive: qisas (cutting the perpetrator's hand) and/or diya (blood money). Note the amounts.
The text says 'half the diya' – that is half of the hand-diya (i.e., 1/4 of full body diya in standard Hanafi amounts).
3
Analyze the first proof (athar)
The passage cites the practice of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i regarding two perpetrators. Understand how this by analogy supports multiple victims being able to share qisas and add diya.
The athar shows that when multiple perpetrators injure one victim, each can be subject to qisas – the converse also applies.
4
Analyze the second proof (hadith)
The hadith of Amr ibn Shu'ayb explicitly states that the blood-money for a hand is half the 'aql. This establishes the amount of diya due.
The hadith also supports that diya for intentional injury where qisas is impossible is on the perpetrator's own wealth, not his 'aqila.
5
Synthesize the underlying principle
Conclude that when qisas cannot fully satisfy all victims due to the singularity of the limb, the Hanafi school combines qisas with diya to accomplish justice.
This is an application of istihsan (juristic preference).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا قَطَعَ وَاحِدٌ يُمْنَى رَجُلَيْنِ فَحَضَرَا فَلَهُمَا أَنْ يَقْطَعَا يَمِينَهُ وَيَأْخُذَا مِنْهُ نِصْفَ الدِّيَةِ يَقْتَسِمَانِهَا نِصْفَيْنِ. وَإِنْ حَضَرَ وَاحِدٌ مِنْهُمَا قَطَعَ يَدَهُ وَلِلْآخِرِ عَلَيْهِ نِصْفُ دِيَةٍ. وجه: (۱) قول التابعى لثبوت وَإِذَا قَطَعَ رَجُلَانِ يَدَ رَجُلٍ عَمْدًا وَقَالَ: لَوْ أَعْلَمُكُمَا تَعَمَّدْتُما لَقَطَعْتُكُمَا. (السنن الكبري للبيهقي، بَابُ الاثْنَيْنِ أَوْ أَكْثَرَ يَقْطَعَانِ يَدَ رَجُلٍ مَعًا، نمبر 15977/ بخاري شريف، بَابُ: إِذَا أَصَابَ قَوْمٌ مِنْ رَجُلٍ هَلْ يُعَاقِبُ أَوْ يَقْتَصُّ مِنْهُمْ كلهم، غير 6897). وجه (۱) قول التابعى لثبوت وَإِنْ حَضَرَ وَاحِدٌ مِنْهُمَا قَطَعَ يَدَهُ: عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ، قَالَ: «مَا كَانَ مِنْ جُرْحٍ مِنَ الْعَمْدِ لَا يُسْتَطَاعُ فِيهِ الْقِصَاصُ، فَهُوَ عَلَى الْجَارِحَ فِي مَالِهِ دُونَ عَاقِلَتِهِ» (مصنف ابن ابي شيبه، الْعَمْدُ الَّذِي لَا يُسْتَطَاعُ فِيهِ الْقِصَاصُ، نمبر 27413). وجه: (٢) الحديث لثبوت وَإِنْ حَضَرَ وَاحِدٌ مِنْهُمَا قَطَعَ يَدَهُ: عَنْ عَمْرِو بْنِ شُعَيْبٍ، عَنْ أَبِيهِ، عَنْ جَدِهِ، قَالَ: كَانَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم يُقَومُ دِيَةَ الْخَطَا عَلَى أَهْلِ الْقُرَى أَرْبَعَ مِائَةِ دِينَارٍ ....... وَفِي الْيَدِ إِذَا قُطِعَتْ نِصْفُ الْعَقْل، وَفِي الرّجُل نِصْفُ الْعَقْل. (سنن ابوداود بَابُ دِيَاتِ الْأَعْضَاءِ، غمبر 4564/ سنن نسائی، ذِكْرُ حَدِيثِ عَمْرُو بْن حَزْمٍ فِي الْعُقُولِ وَاخْتِلَافِ النَّاقِلِينَ له، غمبر 4858)
And if one person cuts the right hand of two men, and they are present, then they both may cut his right hand and take from him half the blood-money, dividing it in half. And if one of them is present, he cuts his hand, and the absent one has half a blood-money against him. Proof: (1) The saying of the follower (Tabi'i) for affirming: 'And if two men cut a man's hand intentionally, he said: If I knew you both had intended, I would have cut your hands.' (Sunan al-Kubra al-Bayhaqi, chapter: Two or more cut a man's hand together, no. 15977; Bukhari, chapter: If a group injure a man, is one punished or all punished? no. 6897). Proof (1) The saying of the follower for affirming 'And if one of them is present, he cuts his hand': from Ibrahim, he said: 'Whatever injury from intentional harm that is not possible to execute qisas for, then it is upon the injurer in his wealth, not upon his 'aqila.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, chapter: Intentional harm impossible to execute qisas for, no. 27413). Proof: (2) The hadith for affirming 'And if one of them is present, he cuts his hand': from Amr ibn Shu'ayb, from his father, from his grandfather, he said: The Messenger of Allah (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) would assess the blood-money of accidental killing upon the people of villages as four hundred dinars... and for the hand when it is cut, half the 'aql (blood-money), and for the leg, half the 'aql. (Sunan Abi Dawud, chapter: Blood-moneys of limbs, no. 4564; Sunan al-Nasa'i, chapter: Mention of 'Amr b. Hazm's hadith on blood-moneys and difference of transmitters, no. 4858).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage from the chapter of injuries (jinayat) presents two closely related rulings of Quduri's matn, each accompanied by its textual proof. In the first case, if a single perpetrator cuts off the right hand of two victims, and both victims are present at the time of retaliation, they have the right to jointly cut off the perpetrator's right hand as qisas (retribution) and also collect half of the full diya for a hand (which is half the full body diya, i.e., 250 dinars) divided equally between them. The rationale is that the hand is a single limb that cannot be divided proportionally in qisas, so a combination of qisas (for the hand) and diya (to compensate the missing share of qisas for the second victim) is required. The first proof cited is the practice of the Tabi'i (likely Ibrahim al-Nakha'i) narrated by Umar (ra?) that if two men cut a man's hand intentionally, the ruler would cut both their hands, indicating that multiple offenders can each receive qisas, which by analogy supports the notion that multiple victims can each claim a portion. In the second case, if only one victim is present, he alone executes the qisas by cutting the perpetrator's hand, and the absent victim is entitled to claim half the full hand diya from the perpetrator's personal property (not from his 'aqila), because qisas cannot be executed for an absent party. The proof for this is a narration from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that for intentional injuries where qisas is impossible, the liability falls on the perpetrator's own wealth. Additionally, a hadith from Amr ibn Shu'ayb establishes that the diya for a hand is half the full blood-money (i.e., half of a full diya, which is 500 dinars or equivalent), confirming the amount of half diya due to the absent victim. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi principle that when qisas is possible, it is executed, but when partial qisas is impossible, diya compensates the deficit.

Key Terms
قِصَاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment in kind for intentional bodily harm or killing.
دِيَة (diyah): Blood-money paid as compensation for bodily injury or death, mainly in cases where qisas is not possible.
عَاقِلَة ('aqila): The male agnates (tribe or family) who pay diya on behalf of a perpetrator for accidental or semi-intentional killings/injuries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario of the passage, if both victims are present, what do they receive?
A) They each cut off the perpetrator's right hand separately.
B) They jointly cut off the perpetrator's right hand and take half the hand-diya divided between them.
C) They take full hand-diya each and the perpetrator goes free.
D) They cut off the perpetrator's left hand as additional qisas.

Key Takeaway
When multiple victims share an indivisible injury, Hanafi law combines qisas with diya to satisfy all claimants.

Slave's Confession to Intentional Killing
Ruling that a slave's confession to intentional killing makes qisas obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a slave's confession to intentional killing makes qisas wajib and cite the Quranic and Companion proofs.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to intentional killing makes qisas wajib; the Quran and Ali's practice confirm it.

In this lesson, we examine a clear ruling from Al-Quduri: when a slave confesses to intentional murder, qisas becomes mandatory upon him. The passage provides two proofs: a Quranic verse that includes slaves in the law of qisas, and a statement from Caliph Ali (ra) that gives the victim's family the choice of execution. Understanding this ruling illustrates the Hanafi commitment to legal equality in criminal liability, even for slaves.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling from the matn
Read the opening sentence: 'When a slave confesses to intentional killing, retribution is mandatory upon him.' This is the legal rule to memorize.
The ruling is unconditional: the slave's confession alone triggers qisas.
2
Identify the Quranic proof
Locate the verse from Surah al-Baqarah (2:178) quoted in the passage. Note the phrase 'the slave for the slave' (العبد بالعبد). Understand that although the verse mentions slave for slave, Hanafi scholars extend it to a slave killing a free person by analogy.
The verse is general in implication, so it covers any intentional killing by a slave.
3
Identify the Companion proof
Find the saying of Ali (ra) cited: 'When a slave kills a free person, the guardians may kill him or spare him.' This shows that qisas is the default, and confession establishes guilt.
This is a qawl sahabi, which is considered a valid proof in Hanafi usul when not contradicted.
4
Explain the Hanafi reasoning
The slave's confession is valid because he is a mukallaf (legally responsible) for criminal acts. The illa (effective cause) for qisas is the act of intentional killing, regardless of status.
This differs from areas where a slave's testimony is limited; criminal confession is treated differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا أَقَرَّ الْعَبْدُ بِقَتْلِ الْعَمْدِ لَزِمَهُ الْقَوَدُ. وجه (1) الآية لثبوت ذلك: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى الْحُرُّ بِالْحُرِّ وَالْعَبْدُ بِالْعَبْدِ وَالْأُنْثَى بِالْأُنْثَى﴾ (سورة البقرة، 2 آية 178). وجه (۲) قول الصحابي لثبوت ذلك: عَنْ عَلِيٍّ، قَالَ: " إِذَا قَتَلَ الْعَبْدُ الْحُرَّ رُفِعَ إِلَى أَوْلِيَاءِ الْمَقْتُولِ، فَإِنْ شَاءُوا قَتَلُوا، وَإِنْ شَاءُوا اسْتَحْيَوْهُ " (السنن الكبري للبيهقي، بَابُ الْعَبْدِ يَقْتُلُ الْحُرَّ، غبر 15961).
And when a slave confesses to intentional killing, retribution (qawad) is mandatory upon him. Proof (1) The verse for affirming that: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution in cases of murder: the free for the free, the slave for the slave, and the female for the female.' (Quran 2:178). Proof (2) The saying of the companion for affirming that: From Ali, he said: 'When a slave kills a free person, the matter is raised to the guardians of the slain; if they wish, they may kill him, and if they wish, they may spare him.' (Sunan al-Kubra al-Bayhaqi, chapter: The slave kills a free person, no. 15961).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling from the matn of Quduri: if a slave confesses to committing intentional murder (qatl al-'amd), then qisas (retributive execution) becomes mandatory upon him, just as it would for a free person. The slave's confession is valid and binding because he is a legally responsible person (mukallaf) for criminal acts. Two proofs are provided. First, the Qur'anic verse (al-Baqarah 2:178) explicitly includes 'the slave for the slave' in the law of qisas, establishing that a slave's life is subject to retribution when he kills another. Although the verse mentions 'slave for slave', it is general in implication, and by analogy (qiyas) a slave who kills a free person also incurs qisas. Second, the practice of Caliph Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) is cited: when a slave kills a free person, the victim's guardians are given the choice between executing the slave or sparing him (presumably accepting diya or pardon). This shows that qisas is the default upon the slave's guilt, and confession establishes guilt. The Hanafi position is that a slave's confession to intentional killing is effective, unlike some other areas where a slave's testimony is limited. The ruling reflects the principle of legal equality in qisas as per the Quran.

Key Terms
إِقْرَار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment of a right or liability, which is binding in judicial proceedings.
قَوَد (qawad): Retribution (qisas) in cases of intentional killing or injury; same as qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the Quranic proof (al-Baqarah 2:178) establish regarding a slave who commits intentional killing?
A) The slave is not subject to qisas because he is property.
B) The slave is subject to qisas only if the victim is also a slave.
C) The slave is subject to qisas, as the verse includes 'the slave for the slave', and by analogy for a free victim.
D) The slave's confession is invalid because he is not legally responsible.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession to intentional killing is valid and makes qisas wajib, supported by Quranic generality and Companion practice.

Liability for Killing Two with One Shot: Qisas and Diya
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an intentional act kills two persons (one intended, one unintended) and apply the distinction between qisas and diya on the 'aqila.

Core Idea
Intentional killing of one yields qisas; unintended killing of another yields diya on the 'aqila.

This passage from Al-Quduri addresses a classic scenario: a single intentional shot that kills both the target and an unintended second victim. The Hanafi ruling differentiates liability based on the nature of each killing. Understanding this distinction helps clarify fundamental principles of homicide liability: intent (qisas) versus error (diya on the 'aqila). We'll examine the ruling, its proof from hadith, and the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two victims
The act is intentionally shooting an arrow at a man. The arrow passes through him and kills a second person. Both die. You must separate the two deaths analytically.
The first victim is the intended target; the second is unintentionally struck.
2
Classify each killing
According to Hanafi fiqh, the first killing is 'amd (intentional) because the act was directed at that person. The second killing is khata' (mistake/accident) because the perpetrator did not intend to kill that person.
The classification determines the type of liability.
3
State the ruling for each victim
For the first (intentional), qisas is wajib (retributive execution). For the second (unintentional), full diya (blood-money) is wajib, but it is payable by the perpetrators 'aqila (male agnates), not from his own wealth.
Qisas is for intentional killing; diya on 'aqila is for accidental killing.
4
Connect to the hadith proof
The hadith of the two women from Hudhayl is cited. In that case, a woman threw a stone killing another woman and her fetus. The Prophet (saw) ruled diya for the fetus (ghurra) and diya for the woman on the perpetrator's 'aqila. Although the woman's killing was also accidental, the principle that a single act can generate two different liabilities is established. The Hanafi school analogizes: here the first killing is intentional (qisas), the second accidental (diya on 'aqila).
The hadith supports the general principle that the 'aqila bears diya in cases of khata'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ رَمَى رَجُلًا عَمْدًا فَنَفَذَ مِنْهُ السَّهْمُ إِلَى آخَرَ فَمَاتَا فَعَلَيْهِ الْقِصَاصُ لِلْأَوَّلِ، وَالدِّيَةُ لِلثَّانِي عَلَى عَاقِلَتِهِ. وجه: (ا) الحديث لثبوت ذلك: أَنَّ أَبَا هُرَيْرَةَ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ قَالَ: «اقْتَتَلَتِ امْرَأَتَانِ مِنْ هُذَيْلٍ، فَرَمَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا الْأُخْرَى بِحَجَرٍ قَتَلَتْهَا وَمَا فِي بَطْنِهَا، فَاخْتَصَمُوا إِلَى النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم، فَقَضَى أَنَّ دِيَةَ جَنِينِهَا غُرَّةٌ، عَبْدٌ أَوْ وَلِيدَةٌ، وَقَضَى دِيَةَ الْمَرْأَةِ عَلَى عَاقِلَتِهَا.» (بخاري شريف، بَابُ جَنِينِ الْمَرْأَةِ وَأَنَّ الْعَقْلَ عَلَى الْوَالِدِ وَعَصَبَةِ الْوَالِدِ لَا عَلَى الْوَلَدِ، نمبر 6910؛ مسلم شريف، بَاب دِيَةِ الجَنِينِ، وَوُجُوبِ الدِّيَةِ فِي قَتْلِ الْخَطَأِ وَشِبْهِ الْعَمْدِ عَلَى عَاقِلَةِ الْجَانِي، نمبر 1682).
And whoever intentionally shoots an arrow at a man, and the arrow penetrates through him to another, and both die, then upon him is retribution for the first, and the blood-money for the second upon his 'aqila. Proof: (1) The hadith for affirming that: Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Two women from Hudhayl fought; one of them threw a stone at the other, killing her and what was in her womb. They brought the case to the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace), and he ruled that the blood-money for her fetus is a slave, male or female, and he ruled that the blood-money of the woman is upon her 'aqila.' (Bukhari, chapter: The fetus of a woman and that the 'aql is upon the father and his asaba, not upon the child, no. 6910; Muslim, chapter: Diya of the fetus and obligation of diya in killing by mistake and quasi-intentional upon the 'aqila of the perpetrator, no. 1682).
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a single intentional act of shooting causes two deaths: the intended target (first victim) and an unintended second victim struck by the arrow after passing through the first. The ruling is that the perpetrator must face qisas (retributive execution) for the first victim, because the killing of the first was intentional ('amd). For the second victim, the killing is considered unintentional (khata'), so the perpetrator must pay the full diya (blood-money), but this diya is payable by his 'aqila (male agnates), as is the rule for accidental killings. The proof is the well-known hadith of the two women from Hudhayl: one woman threw a stone at another, killing her and her fetus. The Prophet (saw) ruled that the diya for the fetus is a slave (ghurra), and the diya for the woman (the primary victim) is upon the 'aqila of the perpetrator. In that case, the woman's killing was considered accidental (as the thrower intended to hit but not necessarily kill?), and the fetus was also accidental. The Hanafi interpretation draws an analogy: here, the first killing is clearly intentional (qisas), while the second is accidental (diya on 'aqila). The underlying principle is that the 'aqila is responsible for diya in cases of khata' (error) and shibh al-'amd (quasi-intentional), but not for pure 'amd. The passage illustrates how a single action can generate multiple layers of liability based on the nature of each resulting death.

Key Terms
خَطَأ (khata'): Accidental or mistaken act causing harm; here, the second killing is classified as khata'.
غُرَّة (ghurra): A slave (male or female) as diya for a fetus; value is half of full diya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the diya for the second victim (killed by the arrow after passing through the first) payable by the 'aqila and not by the perpetrator directly?
A) Because the perpetrator had no intention to kill the second victim, so it is classified as khata' (accident).
B) Because the 'aqila is always responsible for diya in all killings.
C) Because the second victim's family waived qisas.
D) Because the perpetrator is too poor to pay.

Key Takeaway
When a single intentional act kills two persons, the intended victim triggers qisas, and the unintended victim triggers diya on the 'aqila because the second killing is classified as khata'.

Rules of Qasamah: Oath Repetition and Excluded Persons
Rulings on qasamah: repeating oaths, excluded persons, and signs of violence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for qasamah, identify who is excluded from taking oaths, and distinguish signs of violence from natural causes.

Core Idea
If fifty oath-takers are unavailable, oaths are repeated until fifty; not everyone qualifies to swear.

Qasamah is a unique mechanism in Hanafi law for resolving homicides with strong circumstantial evidence. This passage from al-Quduri's matn details the procedure when the locality lacks fifty eligible oath-takers and specifies which individuals cannot participate. It also distinguishes between marks of violence and natural bleeding, and assigns liability when a body is found on a mount.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on repeating oaths
If the people of the locality cannot produce fifty men to swear, the oaths are repeated on each eligible man until the total reaches fifty. This ensures the required number is fulfilled.
Repetition applies only when fewer than fifty eligible men exist.
2
List the persons excluded from qasamah
The text excludes children, madmen, women, slaves, mudabbirs (slaves with deferred freedom), and mukatabs (slaves in manumission contracts). These are considered unreliable or incapable of bearing such serious oaths.
The exclusion is based on lack of legal capacity (ahliyyah) or dependency.
3
Distinguish signs of violence from natural causes
If the body has no external mark, no qasamah or blood money is due. Blood flowing from natural openings (nose, mouth, anus) is not a sign of violence. However, blood from eyes or ears indicates foul play.
The distinction is based on customary understanding of injury types.
4
Analyze the case of a body on a mount
If a slain person is found on a mount driven by a man, the blood money is on the aqila of that man, not on the people of the locality. The driver is presumed responsible.
This shifts liability away from the locality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْقَسَامَةِ
فَإِنْ لَمْ يُحْمِلْ أَهْلُ الْمَحَلَّةِ خَمْسِينَ كُرِّرَتْ الْأَيْمَانُ عَلَيْهِمْ حَتَّى تَتِمَّ خَمْسِينَ يَمِينًا وَلَا يَدْخُلُ فِي الْقَسَامَةِ صَبِيٌّ وَلَا مَجْنُونٌ وَلَا امْرَأَةٌ وَلَا عَبْدٌ وَلَا مُدَبَّرٌ وَلَا مُكَاتَبٌ وَإِنْ وُجِدَ مَيْتًا لَا أَثَرَ بِهِ فَلَا قَسَامَةَ وَلَا دِيَةَ وَكَذَلِكَ إِذَا كَانَ الدَّمُ يَسِيلُ مِنْ أَنْفِهِ أَوْ دُبُرِهِ أَوْ فَمِهِ وَإِنْ كَانَ يَخْرُجُ مِنْ عَيْنَيْهِ أَوْ أُذُنَيْهِ فَهُوَ قَتِيلٌ وَإِذَا وُجِدَ الْقَتِيلُ عَلَى دَابَّةٍ يَسُوقُهَا رَجُلٌ فَالدِّيَةُ عَلَى عَاقِلَتِهِ دُونَ أَهْلِ الْمَحَلَّةِ
Chapter of Qasamah. If the people of the locality do not produce fifty, the oaths are repeated upon them until fifty oaths are completed. No child, madman, woman, slave, mudabbir, or mukatib enters into qasamah. If a dead body is found without any mark, there is no qasamah and no blood money. Likewise, if blood flows from his nose, anus, or mouth; but if it comes out of his eyes or ears, then he is a slain person. And if the slain person is found on a mount that a man is driving, the blood money is upon his aqila, not upon the people of the locality.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage from the chapter of qasamah in al-Quduri's matn presents several rulings. First, regarding the number of oath-takers: if the inhabitants of the locality where a body is found cannot muster fifty men to swear, the oaths are repeated on each man until the total reaches fifty. Second, certain persons are excluded from participating in the oath-taking: a child (who has not reached puberty), a madman (lacking sanity), a woman, a slave, a mudabbir (a slave whose freedom is deferred after the master's death), and a mukatab (a slave in a contract of manumission). These individuals are not considered reliable or capable in such serious oaths. Third, the text distinguishes between a body with signs of violence and one without. If the body has no visible mark of injury, no qasamah is required and no blood money is due. Similarly, if blood flows from natural openings like the nose, anus, or mouth, it is not considered a violent death. However, if blood exits from the eyes or ears, that is a clear sign of foul play and the person is deemed a homicide victim. Fourth, if the dead body is found on an animal being driven by a man, the diyah is imposed on the aqila (the 'aasaba or male relatives) of that man, not on the inhabitants of the locality. The proofs for these rulings are provided in the annotations that follow in the text, referencing athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, al-Thawri, and Shuraih.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasāmah): An oath taken fifty times by the people of the locality where a dead body is found with signs of violence, to exonerate themselves from liability for the blood money.
عاقلة (ʿāqilah): The male relatives on the father's side ('asaba) who are responsible for paying blood money in cases of unintentional homicide.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whose master has declared that he will be free upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a sign of violence that triggers qasamah?
A) Blood flowing from the nose
B) Blood flowing from the ears
C) A visible wound
D) Blood flowing from the eyes

Key Takeaway
Qasamah requires fifty oaths from eligible free adult males of the locality; if fewer are available, oaths are repeated; certain persons are excluded, and only signs of violence trigger the process.

Determining Qasamah Liability by Location
Rulings on which party bears qasamah based on where the body is found

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for qasamah and diyah for each location type (house, ship, mosque, main road, wilderness, between villages) and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding residents.

Core Idea
Where a slain body is found dictates who swears and who pays—homeowner, community, state treasury, or no one.

When a corpse is discovered, Islamic law does not leave its blood unaccounted for. The Hanafi school provides precise rules linking the location to the responsible party. This lesson examines al-Quduri's rulings on seven distinct scenarios, clarifying the underlying reasoning and the proof from the Sunnah for public liability.

Guided Path
1
Locate the location categories
Identify each place mentioned in the passage: house of someone, ship, mosque of a locality, grand mosque/main street, uninhabited wilderness, and the area between two villages.
2
State the ruling for each
For each location, determine who bears the qasamah (oaths) and who pays the diyah (blood money). Note the exceptions: grand mosque/street → no qasamah, diyah from bayt al-mal; wilderness → hadar (no liability).
The rulings are absolute; no conditions of lawth are mentioned beyond the location itself.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi disagreement
The text says residents do not enter qasamah with owners according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Hanifa held that residents share the qasamah. Understand the basis of each view and which is mufta bihi.
The Sahibayn's view is the relied-upon position ('indahuma).
4
Understand the concept of ahl al-khattah
Liability for qasamah in a house is on the original settlers (ahl al-khattah), not later purchasers. Even if only one original settler remains, he alone swears. If none remain, the text implies the liability may shift, but the exact ruling is not given here.
This rule applies only to houses originally allocated by the state; modern purchased homes may analogize differently.
5
Connect to the dalil
The hadith of Sahl ibn Abi Hathmah shows the Prophet ﷺ paid diyah from the state treasury when the killer was unknown and no party was implicated. This is the basis for the bayt al-mal ruling in public spaces.
The same hadith also supports the principle that qasamah requires lawth; here location provides the lawth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ وُجِدَ الْقَتِيلُ فِي دَارِ إِنْسَانٍ فَالْقَسَامَةُ عَلَيْهِ وَالدِّيَةُ عَلَى عَاقِلَتِهِ وَلَا يَدْخُلُ السُّكَّانُ فِي الْقَسَامَةِ مَعَ الْمُلَّاكِ عِنْدَهُمَا وَهِيَ عَلَى أَهْلِ الْخُطَّةِ دُونَ الْمُشْتَرِينَ وَلَوْ بَقِيَ مِنْهُمْ وَاحِدٌ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَبْقَ وَاحِدٌ مِنْهُمْ وَإِذَا وُجِدَ قَتِيلٌ فِي الدَّارِ فَالْقَسَامَةُ عَلَى رَبِّ الدَّارِ وَإِنْ وُجِدَ الْقَتِيلُ فِي سَفِينَةٍ فَالْقَسَامَةُ عَلَى مَنْ فِيهَا مِنْ الرَّكَابِ وَالْمَلَّاحِينَ وَإِنْ وُجِدَ الْقَتِيلُ فِي مَسْجِدِ مَحَلَّةٍ فَالْقَسَامَةُ عَلَى أَهْلِهَا وَإِنْ وُجِدَ فِي الْجَامِعِ أَوْ الشَّارِعِ الْأَعْظَمِ فَلَا قَسَامَةَ فِيهِ وَلَمْ يُعْرَفْ قَاتِلُهُ فَالدِّيَةُ فِي بَيْتِ الْمَالِ وَإِنْ وُجِدَ فِي بَرِّيَّةٍ لَيْسَ بِقُرْبِهِمَا عِمَارَةٌ فَهُوَ هَدَرٌ وَإِنْ وُجِدَ بَيْنَ قَرْيَتَيْنِ كَانَ عَلَى أَقْرَبِهِمَا الْقَسَامَةُ وَالدِّيَةُ
And if the slain person is found in the house of someone, the qasamah is upon him and the blood money is upon his aqila. And the residents do not enter into qasamah with the owners according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); and it is upon the original settlers (ahl al-khattah) not the later purchasers, even if only one of them remains, and if none of them remains. And if a slain person is found in the house, the qasamah is upon the owner of the house. And if the slain person is found in a ship, the qasamah is upon those in it of the passengers and sailors. And if the slain person is found in a mosque of a locality, the qasamah is upon its inhabitants. And if found in the grand mosque (jami') or the main street (al-shari' al-a'zam), there is no qasamah in it, and if his killer is not known, the blood money is from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). And if found in a wilderness where there is no habitation near them, then it is of no value (hadar). And if found between two villages, then qasamah and blood money are upon the closer of the two.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on qasamah, specifying the party responsible based on where the body is found. If a corpse is inside a house, the owner bears the qasamah and his aqila pays the diyah; residents who are not owners do not share the oath according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn). The liability is on the original settlers (ahl al-khattah) of the house, not those who later purchased property; if only one original settler remains, he alone swears. For a ship, the qasamah is on the passengers and crew collectively. For a mosque of a neighborhood, the inhabitants of that locality swear. However, if the body is found in the grand mosque (jami') or the main public road, no qasamah is required; if the killer is unknown, the diyah is paid from the state treasury (bayt al-mal) to ensure blood is not wasted. For a wilderness with no nearby settlement, the death is 'hadar' (without liability). If found between two villages, the closer village bears the qasamah and diyah. The proofs cited include athar from al-Sha'bi, al-Thawri, 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, and hadith from Bukhari and Muslim regarding the precedent of the Prophet ﷺ paying diyah from the charity camels for a victim found between two tribes.

Key Terms
خطة (khuttah): The original allocation of land or property; 'ahl al-khattah' are the first settlers who were given the land, as opposed to later purchasers.
هدر (hadar): A death for which no blood money or qasamah is due; the victim's blood is wasted.
بيت المال (bayt al-māl): The public treasury of the Islamic state, from which diyah is paid when no specific party is responsible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dead body is found in the main street of a city and the killer is unknown, what happens according to the passage?
A) The people of the street swear qasamah
B) The blood money is from the public treasury
C) The owner of the nearest house pays
D) No blood money is due

Key Takeaway
The location of the slain body is the primary determinant for qasamah and diyah liability in Hanafi law, with public spaces shifting the burden to the state and uninhabited wilderness extinguishing it entirely.

Diyah for Unintentional Killing: Liability of the 'Aqilah
Opening of the Book of Ma'aqil: ruling on diyah liability and definition of 'aqilah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that diyah for shibh 'amd and khata' is on the 'aqilah, and identify the definition of 'aqilah as the people of the diwan with supporting evidence.

Core Idea
Blood money for accidental killing is paid by the tribe, not the killer — spreading liability binds the community and restrains the individual.

In the Book of Ma'aqil, Al-Quduri establishes a key principle of Hanafi criminal law: the diyah (blood money) for quasi-intentional and accidental homicide is borne collectively by the 'aqilah, not by the killer alone. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the definition of 'aqilah as 'ahl al-diwan' (people of the military register), and the multiple evidences that support this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
What does the matn say about who pays the diyah for shibh 'amd and khata'? Locate the sentence that declares the liability.
The matn states: 'الدية في شبه العمد والخطأ ... على العاقلة'.
2
Define the 'aqilah
How does the matn define the 'aqilah? What does 'ahl al-diwan' mean in historical context?
The text says: 'والعاقلة أهل الديوان' — the 'aqilah are the people registered for military stipends.
3
Examine the evidence
List the four pieces of evidence (wujuh) cited in the commentary and classify each as hadith, treaty document, tabi'i saying, or sahabi practice.
Evidence (1) is a hadith from Jabir about blood money on every family; (2) is the Prophetic treaty from Umar's document; (3) is Ibrahim al-Nakha'i's statement; (4) is an incomplete saying of Jabir about Umar's practice.
4
Understand the rationale
Why does the school place liability on the collective? What is the wisdom according to the Urdu principle given at the end?
The Urdu principle explains that 'ma'aqil' derives from 'aql (to restrain), because the family's payment restrains the killer from repeating mistakes.
5
Apply the ruling
Consider a modern scenario: a soldier in a registered army accidentally kills a civilian. How would the Hanafi rule apply?
The soldier's 'aqilah would be his registered unit (ahl al-diwan), who would collectively pay the diyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الْمَعَاقِلِ
قَالَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ: الدِّيَةُ فِي شِبْهِ الْعَمْدِ وَالْخَطَأِ، وَكُلُّ دِيَةٍ وَجَبَتْ بِنَفْسِ الْقَتْلِ عَلَى الْعَاقِلَةِ، وَالْعَاقِلَةُ أَهْلُ الدِّيوَانِ.
وَجْهٌ: (١) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ الدِّيَةِ فِي شِبْهِ الْعَمْدِ: «وَأَنَّ الْعَقْلَ عَلَى عَصَبَتِهَا». (بخاري شريف، بَابُ جَنِينِ الْمَرْأَةِ وَأَنَّ الْعَقْلَ عَلَى الْوَالِدِ وَعَصَبَةِ الْوَالِدِ لَا عَلَى الْوَلَدِ، نَمْبَر ٦٩٠٩ / مسلم شريف، بَابُ دِيَةِ الْجَنِينِ وَوُجُوبِ الدِّيَةِ فِي قَتْلِ الْخَطَأِ وَشِبْهِ الْعَمْدِ عَلَى عَاقِلَةِ الْجَانِي، نَمْبَر ١٦٨١)
وَجْهٌ: (١) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَالْعَاقِلَةُ أَهْلُ الدِّيوَانِ: أَنَّهُ سَمِعَ جَابِرَ بْنَ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ يَقُولُ: كَتَبَ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ عَلَى كُلِّ بَطْنٍ عُقُولَهُ. (مسلم شريف بَابُ تَحْرِيمِ تَوَلِّي الْعَتِيقِ غَيْرَ مَوَالِيهِ، نَمْبَر ١٥٠٧ / سنن نسائي، كَمْ دِيَةُ شِبْهِ الْعَمْدِ وَذِكْرُ الِاخْتِلَافِ عَلَى أَيُّوبَ فِي حَدِيثِ الْقَاسِمِ بْنِ ... نَمْبَر ٤٣٩١)
وَجْهٌ: (٢) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَالْعَاقِلَةُ أَهْلُ الدِّيوَانِ: حَدَّثَنِي عُثْمَانُ بْنُ مُحَمَّدِ بْنِ عُثْمَانَ بْنِ الْأَخْنَسِ بْنِ شَرِيقٍ، قَالَ: أَخَذْتُ مِنْ آلِ عُمَرَ بْنِ الْخَطَّابِ هَذَا الْكِتَابَ، كَانَ مَقْرُونًا بِكِتَابِ الصَّدَقَةِ الَّذِي كَتَبَ عُمَرُ لِلْعُمَّالِ: «بِسْمِ اللَّهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ، هَذَا كِتَابٌ مِنْ مُحَمَّدٍ النَّبِيِّ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ بَيْنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَالْمُؤْمِنِينَ مِنْ قُرَيْشٍ وَيَثْرِبَ وَمَنْ تَبِعَهُمْ فَلَحِقَ بِهِمْ وَجَاهَدَ مَعَهُمْ، أَنَّهُمْ أُمَّةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ دُونَ النَّاسِ، الْمُهَاجِرُونَ مِنْ قُرَيْشٍ عَلَى رَبْعَتِهِمْ يَتَعَاقَلُونَ بَيْنَهُمْ وَهُمْ يَفْدُونَ عَانِيَهُمْ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَالْقِسْطِ بَيْنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ، وَبَنُو عَوْفٍ عَلَى رَبْعَتِهِمْ يَتَعَاقَلُونَ مَعَاقِلَهُمُ الْأُولَى، وَكُلُّ طَائِفَةٍ تَفْدِي عَانِيَهَا بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَالْقِسْطِ بَيْنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ» ثُمَّ ذَكَرَ عَلَى هَذَا النَّسَقِ بَنِي الْحَارِثِ، ثُمَّ بَنِي سَاعِدَةَ، ثُمَّ بَنِي جُشَمَ، ثُمَّ بَنِي النَّجَّارِ، ثُمَّ بَنِي عَمْرِو بْنِ عَوْفٍ، ثُمَّ بَنِي النَّبِيتِ، ثُمَّ بَنِي الْأَوْسِ، ثُمَّ قَالَ: «وَإِنَّ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ لَا يَتْرُكُونَ مُفْرَحًا مِنْهُمْ أَنْ يُعْطُوهُ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ فِي فِدَاءٍ أَوْ عَقْلٍ». (السنن الكبرى للبيهقي، بَابُ الْعَاقِلَةِ، غَيْرَ ١٦٣٦٩)
وَجْهٌ: (٣) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَالْعَاقِلَةُ أَهْلُ الدِّيوَانِ: عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ، قَالَ: «الْعَقْلُ عَلَى أَهْلِ الدِّيوَانِ». (مصنف ابن أبي شيبة، الْعَقْلُ عَلَى مَنْ هُوَ، غَيْرَ ٢٧٣٢٣)
وَجْهٌ: (٤) قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَالْعَاقِلَةُ أَهْلُ الدِّيوَانِ: عَنْ جَابِرٍ، قَالَ: «لَمَّا وَلِيَ عُمَرُ الْخِلَافَةَ فَرَضَ ... (ناقص)
أُصُولٌ: «مَعَاقِلُ» عَقْلٍ مُشْتَقَّةٌ، رَوْکْنَا، عَقْلٌ آمَدٍ، جَبِ خَانْدَانِ وَالَهُ قَتْلِ خَطَا وَقَتْلِ شِبْهِ عَمْدٍ كِي دِيتَ أَدَا كَرْتَنْ هَیں، قَاتِلَ كُو طَعْنَةً دِیکَرْ بَارْ بَارْ أَيْسِي غَلَطِي سَے رَوَکْتَنْ هَیں۔
Book of Ma'aqil. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: The diyah for quasi-intentional killing (shibh 'amd) and accidental killing (khata') and every diyah that becomes obligatory due to the killing itself is upon the 'aqilah (tribal group). And the 'aqilah are the people of the diwan (military register). Evidence: (1) The hadith for establishing the diyah for quasi-intentional killing: 'And that the 'aql (blood money) is upon her 'asabah (agnatic relatives).' (Bukhari, chapter on the foetus of a woman; Muslim, chapter on diyah for the foetus) Evidence: (1) The hadith for establishing that the 'aqilah are the people of the diwan: That he heard Jabir b. 'Abdullah say: The Prophet (peace be upon him) wrote upon every family (batn) their 'uqul (blood moneys). (Muslim; Nasa'i) Evidence: (2) The hadith for establishing that the 'aqilah are the people of the diwan: 'Uthman b. Muhammad narrated... a document from 'Umar b. al-Khattab containing the Prophetic treaty: '... the Muhajirun from Quraysh continue in their condition, paying blood money among themselves... and Banu 'Awf pay their first blood moneys...' (Bayhaqi) Evidence: (3) The saying of a Tabi'i: Ibrahim said: 'The 'aql is upon the people of the diwan.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba) Evidence: (4) The saying of a Sahabi: Jabir said: 'When 'Umar became caliph, he prescribed... (incomplete)' Principles: 'Ma'aqil' is derived from 'aql (to restrain, intelligence). When the family pays the diyah for accidental killing or quasi-intentional killing, they restrain the killer from repeating such mistakes by reproaching him.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Ma'aqil (plural of 'aqilah) in Al-Quduri's matn. It establishes two core rulings: first, that the diyah (blood money) for quasi-intentional killing (shibh 'amd) and accidental killing (khata') is not paid by the killer individually but by his 'aqilah—the group of agnatic relatives or, in an organized state, the people registered in the diwan (the military/pension register). Second, it defines the 'aqilah as 'ahl al-diwan' (people of the register), meaning those who are registered for military allowances, which in later times became the basis for determining who shares liability. The commentary provides four pieces of evidence (wujuh) for this definition: (1) a hadith from Jabir that the Prophet assigned blood money to each 'batn' (family subdivision), implying collective liability; (2) a longer document from the Prophetic covenant recorded by 'Umar showing that tribes paid blood money collectively; (3) the statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, a Tabi'i, that the 'aql is upon the people of the diwan; and (4) an incomplete saying of Jabir about 'Umar's practice. The Urdu principle explains the etymology: 'ma'aqil' comes from 'aql (to restrain), because when the family pays the diyah for the killer's mistake, they repeatedly reproach him, thereby restraining him from future errors.

Key Terms
المعاقل (al-ma‘āqil): Plural of 'aqilah; the collective group responsible for paying diyah for unintentional homicides, typically agnatic relatives or those registered in the diwan.
العاقلة (al-‘āqilah): The 'aqilah is the group (tribe, family, or registered military personnel) that pays the diyah; in the text, defined specifically as 'ahl al-dīwān' (people of the register) in the context of a state structure.
الديوان (ad-dīwān): The military or administrative register; during the caliphate of 'Umar, a register was established for stipends ('ata'), and those registered were considered the 'aqilah for their members.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who bears the diyah for accidental killing (khata')?
A) The killer alone
B) The killer's immediate family only
C) The 'aqilah, defined as the people of the diwan or tribal group
D) The state treasury

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the diyah for accidental and quasi-intentional killing is a collective responsibility of the 'aqilah, defined as the people of the diwan, binding the community to support the individual and prevent future harm.
